<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery_and_exploration">Discovery and exploration</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Discovery_and_exploration_of_the_Solar_System" title="Discovery and exploration of the Solar System">Discovery and exploration of the Solar System</a></div>
<p>For many thousands of years, humanity, with a few notable exceptions, did not recognize the existence of the Solar System. People believed the Earth to be stationary at the center of the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> and categorically different from the divine or ethereal objects that moved through the sky. Although the Greek philosopher <a href="/wiki/Aristarchus_of_Samos" title="Aristarchus of Samos">Aristarchus of Samos</a> had speculated on a heliocentric reordering of the cosmos,<sup id="cite_ref-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-0"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nicolaus_Copernicus" title="Nicolaus Copernicus">Nicolaus Copernicus</a> was the first to develop a mathematically predictive heliocentric system. His 17th-century successors, <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">Johannes Kepler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">Isaac Newton</a>, developed an understanding of <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a> which led to the gradual acceptance of the idea that the Earth moves around the Sun and that the planets are governed by the same physical laws that governed the Earth. In more recent times, improvements in the telescope and the use of <a href="/wiki/Unmanned_spacecraft" title="Unmanned spacecraft" class="mw-redirect">unmanned spacecraft</a> have enabled the investigation of geological phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Mountains" title="Mountains" class="mw-redirect">mountains</a> and <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">craters</a>, and seasonal meteorological phenomena such as <a href="/wiki/Clouds" title="Clouds" class="mw-redirect">clouds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dust_storms" title="Dust storms" class="mw-redirect">dust storms</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_caps" title="Ice caps" class="mw-redirect">ice caps</a> on the other planets.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg/400px-Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg.png" width="400" height="400" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Oort_cloud_Sedna_orbit.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbits</a> of the bodies in the Solar System to scale (clockwise from top left)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The principal component of the Solar System is the Sun, a <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> <a href="/wiki/Stellar_classification" title="Stellar classification">G2</a> <a href="/wiki/Star" title="Star">star</a> that contains 99.86 percent of the system's known mass and dominates it gravitationally.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup> The Sun's four largest orbiting bodies, the <a href="/wiki/Gas_giants" title="Gas giants" class="mw-redirect">gas giants</a>, account for 99 percent of the remaining mass, with Jupiter and Saturn together comprising more than 90 percent.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Cnone"><a href="#endnote_Cnone">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>Most large objects in orbit around the Sun lie near the plane of Earth's orbit, known as the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. The planets are very close to the ecliptic while comets and <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a> objects are frequently at significantly greater angles to it.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup> All the planets and most other objects also orbit with the Sun's rotation (counter-clockwise, as viewed from above the Sun's north pole). There are exceptions, such as <a href="/wiki/Halley%27s_Comet" title="Halley's Comet">Halley's Comet</a>.</p>
<p>The overall structure of the charted regions of the Solar System consists of the Sun, four relatively small inner planets surrounded by a belt of rocky asteroids, and four gas giants surrounded by the outer Kuiper belt of icy objects. Astronomers sometimes informally divide this structure into separate regions. The <i>inner Solar System</i> includes the four terrestrial planets and the main asteroid belt. The <i>outer Solar System</i> is beyond the asteroids, including the four gas giant planets.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup> Since the discovery of the Kuiper belt, the outermost parts of the Solar System are considered a distinct region consisting of the objects beyond Neptune.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>6<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler's laws of planetary motion">Kepler's laws of planetary motion</a> describe the orbits of objects about the Sun. According to Kepler's laws, each object travels along an <a href="/wiki/Ellipse" title="Ellipse">ellipse</a> with the Sun at one <a href="/wiki/Focus_(geometry)" title="Focus (geometry)">focus</a>. Objects closer to the Sun (with smaller <a href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a>) travel more quickly, as they are more affected by the Sun's gravity. On an elliptical orbit, a body's distance from the Sun varies over the course of its year. A body's closest approach to the Sun is called its <i><a href="/wiki/Perihelion" title="Perihelion" class="mw-redirect">perihelion</a></i>, while its most distant point from the Sun is called its <i><a href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion" class="mw-redirect">aphelion</a></i>. The orbits of the planets are nearly circular, but many comets, asteroids and Kuiper belt objects follow highly elliptical orbits.</p>
<p>Due to the vast distances involved, many representations of the Solar System show orbits the same distance apart. In reality, with a few exceptions, the farther a planet or belt is from the Sun, the larger the distance between it and the previous orbit. For example, Venus is approximately 0.33&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU)<sup class="reference" id="ref_Dnone"><a href="#endnote_Dnone">[d]</a></sup> farther out from the Sun than Mercury, while Saturn is 4.3&#160;AU out from Jupiter, and Neptune lies 10.5&#160;AU out from Uranus. Attempts have been made to determine a correlation between these orbital distances (for example, the <a href="/wiki/Titius-Bode_law" title="Titius-Bode law" class="mw-redirect">Titius-Bode law</a>),<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> but no such theory has been accepted.</p>
<p>Most of the planets in the Solar System possess secondary systems of their own, being orbited by planetary objects called <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">natural satellites</a>, or moons (two of which are larger than the planet <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>), or, in the case of the four <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">gas giants</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">planetary rings</a>; thin bands of tiny particles that orbit them in unison. Most of the largest natural satellites are in <a href="/wiki/Synchronous_rotation" title="Synchronous rotation">synchronous rotation</a>, with one face permanently turned toward their parent.</p>
<p>The objects of the inner Solar System are composed mostly of <i>rock</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak1995_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak1995-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> the collective name for compounds with high melting points, such as <a href="/wiki/Silicates" title="Silicates" class="mw-redirect">silicates</a>, iron or nickel, that remained solid under almost all conditions in the <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_nebula" title="Protoplanetary nebula">protoplanetary nebula</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak2000_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak2000-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Podolak2000_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak2000-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Jupiter and Saturn are composed mainly of <i>gases</i>, the astronomical term for materials with extremely low melting points and high <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Molecular_hydrogen" title="Molecular hydrogen" class="mw-redirect">molecular hydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, which were always in the gaseous phase in the nebula.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak2000_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak2000-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> <i>Ices</i>, like <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak1995_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak1995-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup> have melting points up to a few hundred kelvins, while their phase depends on the ambient pressure and temperature.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak2000_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak2000-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> They can be found as ices, liquids, or gases in various places in the Solar System, while in the nebula they were either in the solid or gaseous phase.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak2000_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak2000-8"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> Icy substances comprise the majority of the satellites of the giant planets, as well as most of Uranus and Neptune (the so-called "<a href="/wiki/Ice_giant" title="Ice giant" class="mw-redirect">ice giants</a>") and the numerous small objects that lie beyond Neptune's orbit.<sup id="cite_ref-Podolak1995_7-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Podolak1995-7"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-zeilik_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zeilik-9"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> Together, gases and ices are referred to as <i><a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">volatiles</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Placxo_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Placxo-10"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sun">Sun</span></h2>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg/220px-Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg" width="220" height="225" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Venustransit_2004-06-08_07-49.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
A <a href="/wiki/Transit_of_Venus" title="Transit of Venus">transit of Venus</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Sun is the Solar System's star, and by far its chief component. Its large mass (332,900 Earth masses)<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup> produces temperatures and densities in its <a href="/wiki/Sun#Core" title="Sun">core</a> great enough to sustain <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">nuclear fusion</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>13<span>]</span></a></sup> which releases enormous amounts of <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, mostly <a href="/wiki/Radiant_energy" title="Radiant energy">radiated</a> into <a href="/wiki/Outer_space" title="Outer space">space</a> as <a href="/wiki/Electromagnetic_radiation" title="Electromagnetic radiation">electromagnetic radiation</a>, peaking in the 400–to–700&#160;nm band we call <a href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light" class="mw-redirect">visible light</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>14<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun is classified as a type G2 <a href="/wiki/Yellow_dwarf" title="Yellow dwarf" class="mw-redirect">yellow dwarf</a>, but this name is misleading as, compared to the majority of stars in <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">our galaxy</a>, the Sun is rather large and bright.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup> Stars are classified by the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung%E2%80%93Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung–Russell diagram">Hertzsprung–Russell diagram</a>, a graph which plots the brightness of stars with their surface <a href="/wiki/Temperature" title="Temperature">temperatures</a>. Generally, hotter stars are brighter. Stars following this pattern are said to be on the <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a>, and the Sun lies right in the middle of it. However, stars brighter and hotter than the Sun are rare, while substantially dimmer and cooler stars, known as <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarfs</a>, are common, making up 85 percent of the stars in the galaxy.<sup id="cite_ref-sun_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sun-14"><span>[</span>15<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span>[</span>16<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>It is believed that the Sun's position on the main sequence puts it in the "prime of life" for a star, in that it has not yet exhausted its store of hydrogen for nuclear fusion. The Sun is growing brighter; early in its history it was 70 percent as bright as it is today.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span>[</span>17<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Sun is a <a href="/wiki/Metallicity#Population_I_stars" title="Metallicity">population I star</a>; it was born in the later stages of the <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Big_Bang" title="Timeline of the Big Bang">universe's evolution</a>, and thus contains more elements heavier than hydrogen and helium ("<a href="/wiki/Metallicity" title="Metallicity">metals</a>" in astronomical parlance) than older population II stars.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span>[</span>18<span>]</span></a></sup> Elements heavier than hydrogen and helium were formed in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_core" title="Solar core">cores</a> of ancient and exploding stars, so the first generation of stars had to die before the universe could be enriched with these atoms. The oldest stars contain few metals, while stars born later have more. This high metallicity is thought to have been crucial to the Sun's developing a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary system</a>, because planets form from accretion of "metals".<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18"><span>[</span>19<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif/220px-Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Heliospheric-current-sheet.gif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">heliospheric current sheet</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Interplanetary_medium">Interplanetary medium</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">Interplanetary medium</a></div>
<p>Along with <a href="/wiki/Sunlight" title="Sunlight">light</a>, the Sun radiates a continuous stream of charged particles (a plasma) known as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>. This stream of particles spreads outwards at roughly 1.5 million kilometres per hour,<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span>[</span>20<span>]</span></a></sup> creating a tenuous atmosphere (the heliosphere) that permeates the Solar System out to at least 100&#160;AU (see <a href="#Heliopause">heliopause</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-Voyager_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> This is known as the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_medium" title="Interplanetary medium">interplanetary medium</a>. <a href="/wiki/Geomagnetic_storm" title="Geomagnetic storm">Geomagnetic storms</a> on the Sun's surface, such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_flare" title="Solar flare">solar flares</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">coronal mass ejections</a>, disturb the heliosphere, creating <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SunFlip_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SunFlip-21"><span>[</span>22<span>]</span></a></sup> The largest structure within the heliosphere is the <a href="/wiki/Heliospheric_current_sheet" title="Heliospheric current sheet">heliospheric current sheet</a>, a spiral form created by the actions of the Sun's rotating magnetic field on the interplanetary medium.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span>[</span>23<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23"><span>[</span>24<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field" title="Earth's magnetic field">Earth's magnetic field</a> stops <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" title="Earth's atmosphere" class="mw-redirect">its atmosphere</a> from being stripped away by the solar wind. Venus and Mars do not have magnetic fields, and as a result, the solar wind causes their atmospheres to gradually bleed away into space.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24"><span>[</span>25<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">Coronal mass ejections</a> and similar events blow magnetic field and huge quantities of material from the surface of the Sun. The interaction of this magnetic field and material with Earth's magnetic field funnels charged particles into the Earth's upper atmosphere, where its interactions create <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" title="Aurora (astronomy)">aurorae</a> seen near the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_magnetic_field#Magnetic_pole" title="Earth's magnetic field">magnetic poles</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cosmic_rays" title="Cosmic rays" class="mw-redirect">Cosmic rays</a> originate outside the Solar System. The heliosphere partially shields the Solar System, and planetary magnetic fields (for those planets that have them) also provide some protection. The density of cosmic rays in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> and the strength of the Sun's magnetic field change on very long timescales, so the level of cosmic radiation in the Solar System varies, though by how much is unknown.<sup id="cite_ref-Langner_et_al_2005_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Langner_et_al_2005-25"><span>[</span>26<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The interplanetary medium is home to at least two disc-like regions of <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>. The first, the <a href="/wiki/Interplanetary_dust_cloud" title="Interplanetary dust cloud">zodiacal dust cloud</a>, lies in the inner Solar System and causes <a href="/wiki/Zodiacal_light" title="Zodiacal light">zodiacal light</a>. It was likely formed by collisions within the asteroid belt brought on by interactions with the planets.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span>[</span>27<span>]</span></a></sup> The second extends from about 10 AU to about 40 AU, and was probably created by similar collisions within the <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span>[</span>28<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28"><span>[</span>29<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_Solar_System">Inner Solar System</span></h2>
<p>The inner Solar System is the traditional name for the region comprising the terrestrial planets and asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-inner_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inner-29"><span>[</span>30<span>]</span></a></sup> Composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a> and metals, the objects of the inner Solar System are relatively close to the Sun; the radius of this entire region is shorter than the distance between Jupiter and Saturn.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inner_planets">Inner planets</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_planet" title="Terrestrial planet">Terrestrial planet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Terrestrial_planet_size_comparisons.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Terrestrial_planet_size_comparisons.jpg/220px-Terrestrial_planet_size_comparisons.jpg" width="220" height="96" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Terrestrial_planet_size_comparisons.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The inner planets. From left to right: <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> (sizes to scale, interplanetary distances not)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four inner or terrestrial planets have dense, <a href="/wiki/Rock_(geology)" title="Rock (geology)">rocky</a> compositions, few or no <a href="/wiki/Natural_satellite" title="Natural satellite">moons</a>, and no <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">ring systems</a>. They are composed largely of <a href="/wiki/Refractory_(astronomy)" title="Refractory (astronomy)" class="mw-redirect">refractory</a> minerals, such as the <a href="/wiki/Silicate" title="Silicate">silicates</a> which form their <a href="/wiki/Crust_(geology)" title="Crust (geology)">crusts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mantle_(geology)" title="Mantle (geology)">mantles</a>, and metals such as <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nickel" title="Nickel">nickel</a>, which form their <a href="/wiki/Planetary_core" title="Planetary core">cores</a>. Three of the four inner planets (Venus, Earth and Mars) have <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheres</a> substantial enough to generate <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a>; all have <a href="/wiki/Impact_crater" title="Impact crater">impact craters</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tectonics" title="Tectonics">tectonic</a> surface features such as <a href="/wiki/Rift_valley" title="Rift valley">rift valleys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">volcanoes</a>. The term <i>inner planet</i> should not be confused with <i><a href="/wiki/Inferior_planet" title="Inferior planet" class="mw-redirect">inferior planet</a></i>, which designates those planets which are closer to the Sun than Earth is (i.e. Mercury and Venus).</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mercury">Mercury</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Mercury_(planet)" title="Mercury (planet)">Mercury</a> (0.4&#160;<a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the Sun) is the closest planet to the Sun and the smallest planet in the Solar System (0.055 Earth masses). Mercury has no natural satellites, and its only known geological features besides impact craters are lobed ridges or <a href="/wiki/Rupes" title="Rupes">rupes</a>, probably produced by a period of contraction early in its history.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span>[</span>31<span>]</span></a></sup> Mercury's almost negligible atmosphere consists of atoms blasted off its surface by the solar wind.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31"><span>[</span>32<span>]</span></a></sup> Its relatively large iron core and thin mantle have not yet been adequately explained. Hypotheses include that its outer layers were stripped off by a giant impact, and that it was prevented from fully accreting by the young Sun's energy.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span>[</span>33<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span>[</span>34<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Venus">Venus</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a> (0.7&#160;AU from the Sun) is close in size to Earth, (0.815 Earth masses) and like Earth, has a thick silicate mantle around an iron core, a substantial atmosphere and evidence of internal geological activity. However, it is much drier than Earth and its atmosphere is ninety times as dense. Venus has no natural satellites. It is the hottest planet, with surface temperatures over 400 <a href="/wiki/Celsius" title="Celsius">°C</a>, most likely due to the amount of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gases</a> in the atmosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span>[</span>35<span>]</span></a></sup> No definitive evidence of current geological activity has been detected on Venus, but it has no magnetic field that would prevent depletion of its substantial atmosphere, which suggests that its atmosphere is regularly replenished by volcanic eruptions.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span>[</span>36<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Earth">Earth</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> (1&#160;AU from the Sun) is the largest and densest of the inner planets, the only one known to have current geological activity, and is the only place in the <a href="/wiki/Universe" title="Universe">universe</a> where <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> is known to exist.<sup id="cite_ref-life_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-life-36"><span>[</span>37<span>]</span></a></sup> Its liquid <a href="/wiki/Hydrosphere" title="Hydrosphere">hydrosphere</a> is unique among the terrestrial planets, and it is also the only planet where <a href="/wiki/Plate_tectonics" title="Plate tectonics">plate tectonics</a> has been observed. Earth's atmosphere is radically different from those of the other planets, having been altered by the presence of life to contain 21% free <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span>[</span>38<span>]</span></a></sup> It has one natural satellite, the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>, the only large satellite of a terrestrial planet in the Solar System.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Mars">Mars</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a> (1.5&#160;AU from the Sun) is smaller than Earth and Venus (0.107 Earth masses). It possesses an atmosphere of mostly <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> with a surface pressure of 6.1 millibars (roughly 0.6 percent that of the Earth's).<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span>[</span>39<span>]</span></a></sup> Its surface, peppered with vast volcanoes such as <a href="/wiki/Olympus_Mons" title="Olympus Mons">Olympus Mons</a> and rift valleys such as <a href="/wiki/Valles_Marineris" title="Valles Marineris">Valles Marineris</a>, shows geological activity that may have persisted until as recently as 2 million years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39"><span>[</span>40<span>]</span></a></sup> Its red colour comes from <a href="/wiki/Iron(III)_oxide" title="Iron(III) oxide">iron oxide</a> (rust) in its soil.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span>[</span>41<span>]</span></a></sup> Mars has two tiny natural satellites (<a href="/wiki/Deimos_(moon)" title="Deimos (moon)">Deimos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phobos_(moon)" title="Phobos (moon)">Phobos</a>) thought to be captured <a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">asteroids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span>[</span>42<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_belt">Asteroid belt</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">Asteroid belt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/InnerSolarSystem-en.png/300px-InnerSolarSystem-en.png" width="300" height="300" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:InnerSolarSystem-en.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Image of the main <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_belt" title="Asteroid belt">asteroid belt</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroids" title="Trojan asteroids" class="mw-redirect">Trojan asteroids</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Asteroid" title="Asteroid">Asteroids</a> are mostly small Solar System bodies<sup class="reference" id="ref_Enone"><a href="#endnote_Enone">[e]</a></sup> composed mainly of <a href="/wiki/Refractory_(astronomy)" title="Refractory (astronomy)" class="mw-redirect">refractory</a> rocky and metallic <a href="/wiki/Mineral" title="Mineral">minerals</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42"><span>[</span>43<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The main asteroid belt occupies the orbit between Mars and Jupiter, between 2.3 and 3.3&#160;AU from the Sun. It is thought to be remnants from the Solar System's formation that failed to coalesce because of the gravitational interference of Jupiter.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span>[</span>44<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Asteroids range in size from hundreds of kilometres across to microscopic. All asteroids save the largest, Ceres, are classified as small Solar System bodies, but some asteroids such as <a href="/wiki/4_Vesta" title="4 Vesta">Vesta</a> and <a href="/wiki/10_Hygiea" title="10 Hygiea">Hygieia</a> may be reclassed as dwarf planets if they are shown to have achieved <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44"><span>[</span>45<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The asteroid belt contains tens of thousands, possibly millions, of objects over one kilometre in diameter.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45"><span>[</span>46<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite this, the total mass of the main belt is unlikely to be more than a thousandth of that of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> The main belt is very sparsely populated; <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">spacecraft</a> routinely pass through without incident. Asteroids with diameters between 10 and 10<sup>−4</sup>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Metre" title="Metre">m</a> are called <a href="/wiki/Meteoroid" title="Meteoroid">meteoroids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span>[</span>48<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ceres">Ceres</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Ceres_(dwarf_planet)" title="Ceres (dwarf planet)">Ceres</a> (2.77&#160;AU) is the largest body in the asteroid belt and is classified as a dwarf planet.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Enone"><a href="#endnote_Enone">[e]</a></sup> It has a diameter of slightly under 1000&#160;km, and a mass large enough for its own gravity to pull it into a spherical shape. Ceres was considered a planet when it was discovered in the 19th century, but was reclassified as an asteroid in the 1850s as further observation revealed additional asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48"><span>[</span>49<span>]</span></a></sup> It was again reclassified in 2006 as a dwarf planet.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Asteroid_groups">Asteroid groups</span></h4>
<p>Asteroids in the main belt are divided into <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_group" title="Asteroid group" class="mw-redirect">asteroid groups</a> and <a href="/wiki/Category:Asteroid_groups_and_families" title="Category:Asteroid groups and families">families</a> based on their orbital characteristics. <a href="/wiki/Asteroid_moon" title="Asteroid moon" class="mw-redirect">Asteroid moons</a> are asteroids that orbit larger asteroids. They are not as clearly distinguished as planetary moons, sometimes being almost as large as their partners. The asteroid belt also contains <a href="/wiki/Main-belt_comet" title="Main-belt comet">main-belt comets</a> which may have been the source of Earth's water.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49"><span>[</span>50<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Trojan_asteroid" title="Trojan asteroid" class="mw-redirect">Trojan asteroids</a> are located in either of Jupiter's <a href="/wiki/Lagrangian_point#L4_and_L5" title="Lagrangian point">L<sub>4</sub> or L<sub>5</sub> points</a> (gravitationally stable regions leading and trailing a planet in its orbit); the term "Trojan" is also used for small bodies in any other planetary or satellite Lagrange point. <a href="/wiki/Hilda_family" title="Hilda family">Hilda asteroids</a> are in a 2:3 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Jupiter; that is, they go around the Sun three times for every two Jupiter orbits.<sup id="cite_ref-Barucci_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barucci-50"><span>[</span>51<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The inner Solar System is also dusted with <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_asteroid" title="Near-Earth asteroid" class="mw-redirect">rogue asteroids</a>, many of which cross the orbits of the inner planets.<sup id="cite_ref-MorbidelliAstIII_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MorbidelliAstIII-51"><span>[</span>52<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_Solar_System">Outer Solar System</span></h2>
<p>The outer region of the Solar System is home to the gas giants and their large moons. Many short period comets, including the <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(planetoid)" title="Centaur (planetoid)" class="mw-redirect">centaurs</a>, also orbit in this region. Due to their greater distance from the Sun, the solid objects in the outer Solar System contain more ices (such as water, ammonia, methane, often called <i>ices</i> in planetary science) than the rocky denizens of the inner Solar System, as the colder temperatures allow these compounds to remain solid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Outer_planets">Outer planets</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gas_giant" title="Gas giant">Gas giant</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg/220px-Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" width="220" height="283" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gas_giants_in_the_solar_system.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
From top to bottom: <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (not to scale)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The four outer planets, or gas giants (sometimes called Jovian planets), collectively make up 99 percent of the mass known to orbit the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Cnone"><a href="#endnote_Cnone">[c]</a></sup> Jupiter and Saturn are each many tens of times the mass of the Earth and consist overwhelmingly of hydrogen and helium; Uranus and Neptune are far less massive (&lt;20 Earth masses) and possess more ices in their makeup. For these reasons, some astronomers suggest they belong in their own category, “ice giants.”<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span>[</span>53<span>]</span></a></sup> All four gas giants have <a href="/wiki/Planetary_ring" title="Planetary ring">rings</a>, although only Saturn's ring system is easily observed from Earth. The term <i>outer planet</i> should not be confused with <i><a href="/wiki/Superior_planet" title="Superior planet" class="mw-redirect">superior planet</a></i>, which designates planets outside Earth's orbit and thus includes both the outer planets and Mars.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Jupiter">Jupiter</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> (5.2&#160;AU), at 318 Earth masses, is 2.5 times the mass of all the other planets put together. It is composed largely of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>. Jupiter's strong internal heat creates a number of semi-permanent features in its atmosphere, such as cloud bands and the <a href="/wiki/Great_Red_Spot" title="Great Red Spot" class="mw-redirect">Great Red Spot</a>.</dd>
<dd>Jupiter has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Jupiter" title="Moons of Jupiter">63 known satellites</a>. The four largest, <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(moon)" title="Ganymede (moon)">Ganymede</a>, <a href="/wiki/Callisto_(moon)" title="Callisto (moon)">Callisto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(moon)" title="Io (moon)">Io</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europa_(moon)" title="Europa (moon)">Europa</a>, show similarities to the terrestrial planets, such as volcanism and internal heating.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53"><span>[</span>54<span>]</span></a></sup> Ganymede, the largest satellite in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Saturn">Saturn</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a> (9.5&#160;AU), distinguished by its extensive <a href="/wiki/Rings_of_Saturn" title="Rings of Saturn">ring system</a>, has several similarities to Jupiter, such as its atmospheric composition and magnetosphere. Although Saturn has 60% of Jupiter's volume, it is less than a third as massive, at 95 Earth masses, making it the least dense planet in the Solar System. The rings of Saturn are made up of small ice and rock particles.</dd>
<dd>Saturn has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Saturn" title="Moons of Saturn">62 confirmed satellites</a>; two of which, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Enceladus_(moon)" title="Enceladus (moon)">Enceladus</a>, show signs of geological activity, though they are largely <a href="/wiki/Cryovolcano" title="Cryovolcano">made of ice</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54"><span>[</span>55<span>]</span></a></sup> Titan, the second largest moon in the Solar System, is larger than Mercury and the only satellite in the Solar System with a substantial atmosphere.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uranus">Uranus</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Uranus" title="Uranus">Uranus</a> (19.6&#160;AU), at 14 Earth masses, is the lightest of the outer planets. Uniquely among the planets, it orbits the Sun on its side; its <a href="/wiki/Axial_tilt" title="Axial tilt">axial tilt</a> is over ninety degrees to the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic" title="Ecliptic">ecliptic</a>. It has a much colder core than the other gas giants, and radiates very little heat into space.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span>[</span>56<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
<dd>Uranus has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Uranus" title="Moons of Uranus">27 known satellites</a>, the largest ones being <a href="/wiki/Titania_(moon)" title="Titania (moon)">Titania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oberon_(moon)" title="Oberon (moon)">Oberon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Umbriel_(moon)" title="Umbriel (moon)">Umbriel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ariel_(moon)" title="Ariel (moon)">Ariel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Miranda_(moon)" title="Miranda (moon)">Miranda</a>.</dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Neptune">Neptune</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a> (30&#160;AU), though slightly smaller than Uranus, is more massive (equivalent to 17 Earths) and therefore more <a href="/wiki/Density" title="Density">dense</a>. It radiates more internal heat, but not as much as Jupiter or Saturn.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span>[</span>57<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
<dd>Neptune has <a href="/wiki/Moons_of_Neptune" title="Moons of Neptune">13 known satellites</a>. The largest, <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a>, is geologically active, with <a href="/wiki/Geyser" title="Geyser">geysers</a> of <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span>[</span>58<span>]</span></a></sup> Triton is the only large satellite with a <a href="/wiki/Retrograde_orbit" title="Retrograde orbit" class="mw-redirect">retrograde orbit</a>. Neptune is accompanied in its orbit by a number of <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planets</a>, termed <a href="/wiki/Neptune_Trojan" title="Neptune Trojan" class="mw-redirect">Neptune Trojans</a>, that are in 1:1 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with it.</dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comets">Comets</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">Comet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Comet_c1995o1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/58/Comet_c1995o1.jpg/220px-Comet_c1995o1.jpg" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Comet_c1995o1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Comet <a href="/wiki/Hale-Bopp" title="Hale-Bopp" class="mw-redirect">Hale-Bopp</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Comets are small Solar System bodies,<sup class="reference" id="ref_Enone"><a href="#endnote_Enone">[e]</a></sup> typically only a few kilometres across, composed largely of volatile ices. They have highly eccentric orbits, generally a perihelion within the orbits of the inner planets and an aphelion far beyond Pluto. When a comet enters the inner Solar System, its proximity to the Sun causes its icy surface to <a href="/wiki/Sublimation_(chemistry)" title="Sublimation (chemistry)" class="mw-redirect">sublimate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ion" title="Ion">ionise</a>, creating a <a href="/wiki/Coma_(cometary)" title="Coma (cometary)">coma</a>: a long tail of gas and dust often visible to the naked eye.</p>
<p>Short-period comets have orbits lasting less than two hundred years. Long-period comets have orbits lasting thousands of years. Short-period comets are believed to originate in the Kuiper belt, while long-period comets, such as <a href="/wiki/Comet_Hale-Bopp" title="Comet Hale-Bopp">Hale-Bopp</a>, are believed to originate in the Oort cloud. Many comet groups, such as the <a href="/wiki/Kreutz_Sungrazers" title="Kreutz Sungrazers">Kreutz Sungrazers</a>, formed from the breakup of a single parent.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span>[</span>59<span>]</span></a></sup> Some comets with <a href="/wiki/Comet#Orbital_characteristics" title="Comet">hyperbolic</a> orbits may originate outside the Solar System, but determining their precise orbits is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-hyperbolic_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hyperbolic-59"><span>[</span>60<span>]</span></a></sup> Old comets that have had most of their volatiles driven out by solar warming are often categorised as asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span>[</span>61<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Centaurs">Centaurs</span></h4>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Centaur_(minor_planet)" title="Centaur (minor planet)">Centaur (minor planet)</a></div>
<p>The centaurs are icy comet-like bodies with a semi-major axis greater than Jupiter (5.5&#160;AU) and less than Neptune (30&#160;AU). The largest known centaur, <a href="/wiki/10199_Chariklo" title="10199 Chariklo">10199 Chariklo</a>, has a diameter of about 250&#160;km.<sup id="cite_ref-spitzer_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-61"><span>[</span>62<span>]</span></a></sup> The first centaur discovered, <a href="/wiki/2060_Chiron" title="2060 Chiron">2060 Chiron</a>, has also been classified as comet (95P) since it develops a coma just as comets do when they approach the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span>[</span>63<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Trans-Neptunian_region">Trans-Neptunian region</span></h2>
<p>The area beyond Neptune, or the "<a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object" title="Trans-Neptunian object">trans-Neptunian region</a>", is still <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Solar_System_exploration" title="Timeline of Solar System exploration">largely unexplored</a>. It appears to consist overwhelmingly of small worlds (the largest having a diameter only a fifth that of the Earth and a mass far smaller than that of the Moon) composed mainly of rock and ice. This region is sometimes known as the "outer Solar System", though others use that term to mean the region beyond the asteroid belt.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kuiper_belt">Kuiper belt</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kuiper_belt" title="Kuiper belt">Kuiper belt</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png/300px-Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png" width="300" height="294" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Outersolarsystem_objectpositions_labels_comp.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Plot of all known Kuiper belt objects, set against the four outer planets</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Kuiper belt, the region's first formation, is a great ring of debris similar to the asteroid belt, but composed mainly of ice.<sup id="cite_ref-physical_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physical-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> It extends between 30 and 50&#160;AU from the Sun. Though it contains at least three dwarf planets, it is composed mainly of small Solar System bodies. However, many of the largest Kuiper belt objects, such as <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/20000_Varuna" title="20000 Varuna">Varuna</a>, and <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, may be reclassified as dwarf planets. There are estimated to be over 100,000 Kuiper belt objects with a diameter greater than 50&#160;km, but the total mass of the Kuiper belt is thought to be only a tenth or even a hundredth the mass of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> Many Kuiper belt objects have multiple satellites,<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65"><span>[</span>66<span>]</span></a></sup> and most have orbits that take them outside the plane of the ecliptic.<sup id="cite_ref-trojan_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-trojan-66"><span>[</span>67<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Kuiper belt can be roughly divided into the "<a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">classical</a>" belt and the <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonances</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-physical_63-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-physical-63"><span>[</span>64<span>]</span></a></sup> Resonances are orbits linked to that of Neptune (e.g. twice for every three Neptune orbits, or once for every two). The first resonance begins within the orbit of Neptune itself. The classical belt consists of objects having no resonance with Neptune, and extends from roughly 39.4&#160;AU to 47.7&#160;AU.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span>[</span>68<span>]</span></a></sup> Members of the classical Kuiper belt are classified as <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">cubewanos</a>, after the first of their kind to be discovered, <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/(15760)_1992_QB1" title="(15760) 1992 QB1">(15760) 1992 QB<sub>1</sub></a></span>, and are still in near primordial, low-eccentricity orbits.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pluto_and_Charon">Pluto and Charon</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
<div><map name="ImageMap_1_1478461954" id="ImageMap_1_1478461954">
<area href="/wiki/Earth" shape="rect" coords="71,187,234,218" alt="The Earth" title="The Earth" />
<area href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" shape="circle" coords="25,45,2" alt="Dysnomia" title="Dysnomia" />
<area href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" shape="circle" coords="38,68,22" alt="Eris" title="Eris" />
<area href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" shape="circle" coords="137,75,9" alt="Charon" title="Charon" />
<area href="/wiki/Pluto" shape="circle" coords="113,69,21" alt="Pluto" title="Pluto" />
<area href="/wiki/Makemake_(dwarf_planet)" shape="circle" coords="195,67,16" alt="Makemake" title="Makemake" />
<area href="/wiki/Haumea_(dwarf_planet)" shape="circle" coords="266,67,17" alt="Haumea" title="Haumea" />
<area href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" shape="circle" coords="37,142,15" alt="Sedna" title="Sedna" />
<area href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" shape="circle" coords="119,142,12" alt="Orcus" title="Orcus" />
<area href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" shape="circle" coords="195,142,12" alt="2007 OR10" title="2007 OR10" />
<area href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" shape="circle" coords="264,142,11" alt="Quaoar" title="Quaoar" />
<area href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" shape="rect" coords="0,0,300,218" alt="File:EightTNOs.png" title="File:EightTNOs.png" /></map><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/EightTNOs.png/300px-EightTNOs.png" width="300" height="218" class="thumbimage" usemap="#ImageMap_1_1478461954" /></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Comparison of <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake_(dwarf_planet)" title="Makemake (dwarf planet)">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea_(dwarf_planet)" title="Haumea (dwarf planet)">Haumea</a>, <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, <a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> (all to scale)</div>
</div>
</div>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a> (39&#160;AU average), a dwarf planet, is the largest known object in the Kuiper belt. When discovered in 1930, it was considered to be the ninth planet; this changed in 2006 with the adoption of a formal <a href="/wiki/Definition_of_planet" title="Definition of planet">definition of planet</a>. Pluto has a relatively eccentric orbit inclined 17 degrees to the ecliptic plane and ranging from 29.7&#160;AU from the Sun at perihelion (within the orbit of Neptune) to 49.5&#160;AU at aphelion.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>, Pluto's largest moon, is sometimes described as part of a <a href="/wiki/Binary_system_(astronomy)" title="Binary system (astronomy)">binary system</a> with Pluto, as the two bodies orbit a <a href="/wiki/Center_of_mass#Barycenter_in_astrophysics_and_astronomy" title="Center of mass">barycenter</a> of gravity above their surfaces (i.e., they appear to "orbit each other"). Beyond Charon, two much smaller moons, <a href="/wiki/Nix_(moon)" title="Nix (moon)">Nix</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydra_(moon)" title="Hydra (moon)">Hydra</a>, orbit within the system.</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>Pluto has a 3:2 <a href="/wiki/Orbital_resonance" title="Orbital resonance">resonance</a> with Neptune, meaning that Pluto orbits twice round the Sun for every three Neptunian orbits. Kuiper belt objects whose orbits share this resonance are called <a href="/wiki/Plutino" title="Plutino">plutinos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fajans_et_al_2001_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fajans_et_al_2001-69"><span>[</span>70<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Haumea_and_Makemake">Haumea and Makemake</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Haumea_(dwarf_planet)" title="Haumea (dwarf planet)">Haumea</a> (43.34&#160;AU average), and <a href="/wiki/Makemake_(dwarf_planet)" title="Makemake (dwarf planet)">Makemake</a> (45.79&#160;AU average), while smaller than Pluto, are the largest known objects in the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object"><i>classical</i></a> Kuiper belt (that is, they are not in a confirmed <a href="/wiki/Resonant_trans-Neptunian_object" title="Resonant trans-Neptunian object">resonance</a> with Neptune). Haumea is an egg-shaped object with two moons. Makemake is the brightest object in the Kuiper belt after Pluto. Originally designated <b>2003 EL<sub>61</sub></b> and <b>2005 FY<sub>9</sub></b> respectively, they were given names and designated dwarf planets in 2008.<sup id="cite_ref-name_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Their orbits are far more inclined than Pluto's, at 28° and 29°.<sup id="cite_ref-Buie_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Buie-71"><span>[</span>72<span>]</span></a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scattered_disc">Scattered disc</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">Scattered disc</a></div>
<p>The scattered disc, which overlaps the Kuiper belt but extends much further outwards, is thought to be the source of short-period comets. Scattered disc objects are believed to have been ejected into erratic orbits by the gravitational influence of <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System#Planetary_migration" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Neptune's early outward migration</a>. Most scattered disc objects (SDOs) have perihelia within the Kuiper belt but aphelia as far as 150&#160;AU from the Sun. SDOs' orbits are also highly inclined to the ecliptic plane, and are often almost perpendicular to it. Some astronomers consider the scattered disc to be merely another region of the Kuiper belt, and describe scattered disc objects as "scattered Kuiper belt objects."<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span>[</span>73<span>]</span></a></sup> Some astronomers also classify centaurs as inward-scattered Kuiper belt objects along with the outward-scattered residents of the scattered disc.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span>[</span>74<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Eris">Eris</span></h4>
<dl>
<dd><a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> (68&#160;AU average) is the largest known scattered disc object, and caused a debate about what constitutes a planet, since it is at least 5% larger than Pluto with an estimated diameter of 2400&#160;km (1500&#160;mi). It is the largest of the known dwarf planets.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74"><span>[</span>75<span>]</span></a></sup> It has one moon, <a href="/wiki/Dysnomia_(moon)" title="Dysnomia (moon)">Dysnomia</a>. Like Pluto, its orbit is highly eccentric, with a perihelion of 38.2&#160;AU (roughly Pluto's distance from the Sun) and an aphelion of 97.6&#160;AU, and steeply inclined to the ecliptic plane.</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Farthest_regions">Farthest regions</span></h2>
<p>The point at which the Solar System ends and interstellar space begins is not precisely defined, since its outer boundaries are shaped by two separate forces: the solar wind and the Sun's gravity. The outer limit of the solar wind's influence is roughly four times Pluto's distance from the Sun; this <i><a href="/wiki/Heliopause" title="Heliopause" class="mw-redirect">heliopause</a></i> is considered the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Voyager_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup> However, the Sun's <a href="/wiki/Hill_sphere" title="Hill sphere">Roche sphere</a>, the effective range of its gravitational dominance, is believed to extend up to a thousand times farther.<sup id="cite_ref-Littmann_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Littmann-75"><span>[</span>76<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heliopause">Heliopause</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Voyager_1_entering_heliosheath_region.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Voyager_1_entering_heliosheath_region.jpg/300px-Voyager_1_entering_heliosheath_region.jpg" width="300" height="202" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Voyager_1_entering_heliosheath_region.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyagers</a> entering the <a href="/wiki/Heliosheath" title="Heliosheath" class="mw-redirect">heliosheath</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The heliosphere is divided into two separate regions. The solar wind travels at roughly 400&#160;km/s until it collides with the interstellar wind; the flow of plasma in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a>. The collision occurs at the <a href="/wiki/Termination_shock" title="Termination shock" class="mw-redirect">termination shock</a>, which is roughly 80–100&#160;AU from the Sun upwind of the interstellar medium and roughly 200&#160;AU from the Sun downwind.<sup id="cite_ref-fahr_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> Here the wind slows dramatically, condenses and becomes more turbulent,<sup id="cite_ref-fahr_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> forming a great oval structure known as the <a href="/wiki/Heliosheath" title="Heliosheath" class="mw-redirect">heliosheath</a>. This structure is believed to look and behave very much like a comet's tail, extending outward for a further 40&#160;AU on the upwind side but tailing many times that distance downwind; but evidence from the Cassini and <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_Boundary_Explorer" title="Interstellar Boundary Explorer">Interstellar Boundary Explorer</a> spacecraft has suggested that it is in fact forced into a bubble shape by the constraining action of the interstellar magnetic field.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Both <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_1" title="Voyager 1">Voyager 1</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Voyager_2" title="Voyager 2">Voyager 2</a></i> are reported to have passed the termination shock and entered the heliosheath, at 94 and 84&#160;AU from the Sun, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span>[</span>79<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span>[</span>80<span>]</span></a></sup> The outer boundary of the heliosphere, the <a href="/wiki/Heliopause" title="Heliopause" class="mw-redirect">heliopause</a>, is the point at which the solar wind finally terminates and is the beginning of interstellar space.<sup id="cite_ref-Voyager_20-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voyager-20"><span>[</span>21<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The shape and form of the outer edge of the heliosphere is likely affected by the <a href="/wiki/Fluid_dynamics" title="Fluid dynamics">fluid dynamics</a> of interactions with the interstellar medium<sup id="cite_ref-fahr_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fahr-76"><span>[</span>77<span>]</span></a></sup> as well as solar magnetic fields prevailing to the south, e.g. it is bluntly shaped with the northern hemisphere extending 9 AU (roughly 900 million miles) farther than the southern hemisphere. Beyond the heliopause, at around 230&#160;AU, lies the <a href="/wiki/Bow_shock" title="Bow shock">bow shock</a>, a plasma "wake" left by the Sun as it travels through the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80"><span>[</span>81<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>No spacecraft have yet passed beyond the heliopause, so it is impossible to know for certain the conditions in local interstellar space. It is expected that <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Voyager_program" title="Voyager program">Voyager spacecraft</a> will pass the heliopause some time in the next decade and transmit valuable data on radiation levels and solar wind back to the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81"><span>[</span>82<span>]</span></a></sup> How well the heliosphere shields the Solar System from cosmic rays is poorly understood. A NASA-funded team has developed a concept of a "Vision Mission" dedicated to sending a probe to the heliosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span>[</span>83<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83"><span>[</span>84<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oort_cloud">Oort cloud</span></h3>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_oort.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Kuiper_oort.jpg/250px-Kuiper_oort.jpg" width="250" height="215" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kuiper_oort.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
An artist's rendering of the Oort Cloud, the Hills Cloud, and the Kuiper belt (inset)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The hypothetical Oort cloud is a spherical cloud of up to a trillion icy objects that is believed to be the source for all long-period comets and to surround the Solar System at roughly 50,000&#160;AU (around 1&#160;<a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-year</a> (LY)), and possibly to as far as 100,000&#160;AU (1.87&#160;LY). It is believed to be composed of comets which were ejected from the inner Solar System by gravitational interactions with the outer planets. Oort cloud objects move very slowly, and can be perturbed by infrequent events such as collisions, the gravitational effects of a passing star, or the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_tide" title="Galactic tide">galactic tide</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal force</a> exerted by the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84"><span>[</span>85<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span>[</span>86<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Sedna">Sedna</span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">90377 Sedna</a> (525.86 AU average) is a large, reddish Pluto-like object with a gigantic, highly elliptical orbit that takes it from about 76&#160;AU at perihelion to 928&#160;AU at aphelion and takes 12,050 years to complete. <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Mike Brown</a>, who discovered the object in 2003, asserts that it cannot be part of the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> or the Kuiper belt as its perihelion is too distant to have been affected by Neptune's migration. He and other astronomers consider it to be the first in an entirely new population, which also may include the object <span style="white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/2000_CR105" title="2000 CR105" class="mw-redirect">2000 CR<sub>105</sub></a></span>, which has a perihelion of 45&#160;AU, an aphelion of 415&#160;AU, and an orbital period of 3,420 years.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86"><span>[</span>87<span>]</span></a></sup> Brown terms this population the "Inner Oort cloud," as it may have formed through a similar process, although it is far closer to the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span>[</span>88<span>]</span></a></sup> Sedna is very likely a dwarf planet, though its shape has yet to be determined with certainty.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Boundaries">Boundaries</span></h3>
<div class="rellink boilerplate seealso">See also: <a href="/wiki/Vulcanoid_asteroid" title="Vulcanoid asteroid">Vulcanoid asteroid</a>, <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">Planets beyond Neptune</a>,&#160;and <a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(star)" title="Nemesis (star)">Nemesis (star)</a></div>
<p>Much of our Solar System is still unknown. The Sun's gravitational field is estimated to dominate the gravitational forces of <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">surrounding stars</a> out to about two light years (125,000&#160;AU). Lower estimates for the radius of the Oort cloud, by contrast, do not place it farther than 50,000&#160;AU.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span>[</span>89<span>]</span></a></sup> Despite discoveries such as Sedna, the region between the Kuiper belt and the Oort cloud, an area tens of thousands of AU in radius, is still virtually unmapped. There are also ongoing studies of the region between Mercury and the Sun.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89"><span>[</span>90<span>]</span></a></sup> Objects may yet be discovered in the Solar System's uncharted regions.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Galactic_context">Galactic context</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg/220px-Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg.png" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Milky_Way_Spiral_Arm.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"><img src="http://bits.wikimedia.org/skins-1.5/common/images/magnify-clip.png" width="15" height="11" alt="" /></a></div>
Location of the Solar System within our <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Solar System is located in the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a> <a href="/wiki/Galaxy" title="Galaxy">galaxy</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Barred_spiral_galaxy" title="Barred spiral galaxy">barred spiral galaxy</a> with a diameter of about 100,000 <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-years</a> containing about 200 billion stars.<sup id="cite_ref-fn9_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fn9-90"><span>[</span>91<span>]</span></a></sup> Our Sun resides in one of the Milky Way's outer spiral arms, known as the <a href="/wiki/Orion_Arm" title="Orion Arm">Orion Arm</a> or Local Spur.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span>[</span>92<span>]</span></a></sup> The Sun lies between 25,000 and 28,000 light years from the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_Centre" title="Galactic Centre" class="mw-redirect">Galactic Centre</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-distance2_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-distance2-92"><span>[</span>93<span>]</span></a></sup> and its speed within the galaxy is about 220 <a href="/wiki/Metre_per_second" title="Metre per second">kilometres per second</a>, so that it completes one revolution every 225–250 million years. This revolution is known as the Solar System's <a href="/wiki/Galactic_year" title="Galactic year">galactic year</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span>[</span>94<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Solar_apex" title="Solar apex">solar apex</a>, the direction of the Sun's path through interstellar space, is near the constellation of <a href="/wiki/Hercules_(constellation)" title="Hercules (constellation)">Hercules</a> in the direction of the current location of the bright star <a href="/wiki/Vega" title="Vega">Vega</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94"><span>[</span>95<span>]</span></a></sup> The plane of the Solar System's ecliptic lies at an angle of about 60° to the <a href="/wiki/Galactic_plane" title="Galactic plane">galactic plane</a>.<sup class="reference" id="ref_Fnone"><a href="#endnote_Fnone">[f]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Solar System's location in the galaxy is very likely a factor in the <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolution</a> of <a href="/wiki/Life" title="Life">life</a> on Earth. Its orbit is close to being circular and is at roughly the same speed as that of the spiral arms, which means it passes through them only rarely. Since spiral arms are home to a far larger concentration of potentially dangerous <a href="/wiki/Supernova" title="Supernova">supernovae</a>, this has given Earth long periods of interstellar stability for life to evolve.<sup id="cite_ref-astrobiology_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> The Solar System also lies well outside the star-crowded environs of the galactic centre. Near the centre, gravitational tugs from nearby stars could perturb bodies in the Oort Cloud and send many comets into the inner Solar System, producing collisions with potentially catastrophic implications for life on Earth. The intense radiation of the galactic centre could also interfere with the development of complex life.<sup id="cite_ref-astrobiology_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-astrobiology-95"><span>[</span>96<span>]</span></a></sup> Even at the Solar System's current location, some scientists have hypothesised that recent supernovae may have adversely affected life in the last 35,000 years by flinging pieces of expelled stellar core towards the Sun as radioactive dust grains and larger, comet-like bodies.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span>[</span>97<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neighbourhood">Neighbourhood</span></h3>
<p>The immediate galactic neighbourhood of the Solar System is known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_Interstellar_Cloud" title="Local Interstellar Cloud">Local Interstellar Cloud</a> or Local Fluff, an area of denser cloud in an otherwise sparse region known as the <a href="/wiki/Local_Bubble" title="Local Bubble">Local Bubble</a>, an hourglass-shaped cavity in the <a href="/wiki/Interstellar_medium" title="Interstellar medium">interstellar medium</a> roughly 300 light years across. The bubble is suffused with high-temperature plasma that suggests it is the product of several recent supernovae.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span>[</span>98<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>There are relatively few <a href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars" title="List of nearest stars">stars within ten light years</a> (95 trillion&#160;km) of the Sun. The closest is the triple star system <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Centauri" title="Alpha Centauri">Alpha Centauri</a>, which is about 4.4 light years away. Alpha Centauri A and B are a closely tied pair of Sun-like stars, while the small <a href="/wiki/Red_dwarf" title="Red dwarf">red dwarf</a> Alpha Centauri C (also known as <a href="/wiki/Proxima_Centauri" title="Proxima Centauri">Proxima Centauri</a>) orbits the pair at a distance of 0.2 light years. The stars next closest to the Sun are the red dwarfs <a href="/wiki/Barnard%27s_Star" title="Barnard's Star">Barnard's Star</a> (at 5.9 light years), <a href="/wiki/Wolf_359" title="Wolf 359">Wolf 359</a> (7.8 light years) and <a href="/wiki/Lalande_21185" title="Lalande 21185">Lalande 21185</a> (8.3 light years). The largest star within ten light years is <a href="/wiki/Sirius" title="Sirius">Sirius</a>, a bright <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> star roughly twice the Sun's mass and orbited by a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a> called Sirius B. It lies 8.6 light years away. The remaining systems within ten light years are the binary red dwarf system <a href="/wiki/Luyten_726-8" title="Luyten 726-8">Luyten 726-8</a> (8.7 light years) and the solitary red dwarf <a href="/wiki/Ross_154" title="Ross 154">Ross 154</a> (9.7 light years).<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98"><span>[</span>99<span>]</span></a></sup> Our closest solitary sun-like star is <a href="/wiki/Tau_Ceti" title="Tau Ceti">Tau Ceti</a>, which lies 11.9 light years away. It has roughly 80 percent the Sun's mass, but only 60 percent its luminosity.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span>[</span>100<span>]</span></a></sup> The closest known <a href="/wiki/Extrasolar_planet" title="Extrasolar planet">extrasolar planet</a> to the Sun lies around the star <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Eridani" title="Epsilon Eridani">Epsilon Eridani</a>, a star slightly dimmer and redder than the Sun, which lies 10.5 light years away. Its one confirmed planet, <a href="/wiki/Epsilon_Eridani_b" title="Epsilon Eridani b">Epsilon Eridani b</a>, is roughly 1.5 times Jupiter's mass and orbits its star every 6.9 years.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span>[</span>101<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:1008px;">
<div class="thumbinner">
