<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation_and_evolution">Formation and evolution</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; width:99%; max-width:608px;">
<div class="thumbinner">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Solar_Life_Cycle.svg" class="image"><img alt="Projected timeline of the Sun's life." src="http://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Solar_Life_Cycle.svg/600px-Solar_Life_Cycle.svg.png" width="600" height="148" /></a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="rellink relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Formation_and_evolution_of_the_Solar_System" title="Formation and evolution of the Solar System">Formation and evolution of the Solar System</a></div>
<p>The Solar System formed from the gravitational collapse of a giant <a href="/wiki/Molecular_cloud" title="Molecular cloud">molecular cloud</a> 4.568 billion years ago.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span>[</span>102<span>]</span></a></sup> This initial cloud was likely several light-years across and probably birthed several stars.<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>As the region that would become the Solar System, known as the <a href="/wiki/Solar_nebula" title="Solar nebula" class="mw-redirect">pre-solar nebula</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103"><span>[</span>104<span>]</span></a></sup> collapsed, conservation of <a href="/wiki/Angular_momentum" title="Angular momentum">angular momentum</a> made it rotate faster. The centre, where most of the mass collected, became increasingly hotter than the surrounding disc.<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> As the contracting nebula rotated, it began to flatten into a spinning <a href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" title="Protoplanetary disc" class="mw-redirect">protoplanetary disc</a> with a diameter of roughly 200&#160;AU<sup id="cite_ref-Arizona_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arizona-102"><span>[</span>103<span>]</span></a></sup> and a hot, dense <a href="/wiki/Protostar" title="Protostar">protostar</a> at the centre.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span>[</span>105<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> At this point in its <a href="/wiki/Stellar_evolution" title="Stellar evolution">evolution</a>, the Sun is believed to have been a <a href="/wiki/T_Tauri_star" title="T Tauri star">T Tauri star</a>. Studies of T Tauri stars show that they are often accompanied by discs of pre-planetary matter with masses of 0.001–0.1 <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">solar masses</a>, with the vast majority of the mass of the nebula in the star itself.<sup id="cite_ref-Kitamara_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kitamara-106"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The planets formed by <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> from this disk.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Within 50 million years, the pressure and density of <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a> in the centre of the protostar became great enough for it to begin <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">thermonuclear fusion</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Yi2001_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Yi2001-108"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> The temperature, reaction rate, pressure, and density increased until <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a> was achieved, with the thermal energy countering the force of gravitational contraction. At this point the Sun became a full-fledged <a href="/wiki/Main_sequence" title="Main sequence">main sequence</a> star.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The Solar System as we know it today will last until the Sun begins its evolution off of the main sequence of the <a href="/wiki/Hertzsprung-Russell_diagram" title="Hertzsprung-Russell diagram" class="mw-redirect">Hertzsprung-Russell diagram</a>. As the Sun burns through its supply of hydrogen fuel, the energy output supporting the core tends to decrease, causing it to collapse in on itself. This increase in pressure heats the core, so it burns even faster. As a result, the Sun is growing brighter at a rate of roughly ten percent every 1.1 billion years.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Around 5.4&#160;billion years from now, the hydrogen in the core of the Sun will have been entirely converted to helium, ending the main sequence phase. As the hydrogen reactions shut down, the core will contract further, increasing pressure and temperature, causing fusion to commence via the helium process. Helium in the core burns at a much hotter temperature, and the energy output will be much greater than during the hydrogen process. At this time, the outer layers of the Sun will expand to roughly up to 260 times its current diameter; the Sun will become a <a href="/wiki/Red_giant" title="Red giant">red giant</a>. Because of its vastly increased surface area, the surface of the Sun will be considerably cooler than it is on the main sequence (2600&#160;K at the coolest).<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Eventually, helium in the core will exhaust itself at a much faster rate than the hydrogen, and the Sun's helium burning phase will be but a fraction of the time compared to the hydrogen burning phase. The Sun is not massive enough to commence fusion of heavier elements, and nuclear reactions in the core will dwindle. Its outer layers will fall away into space, leaving a <a href="/wiki/White_dwarf" title="White dwarf">white dwarf</a>, an extraordinarily dense object, half the original mass of the Sun but only the size of the Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> The ejected outer layers will form what is known as a <a href="/wiki/Planetary_nebula" title="Planetary nebula">planetary nebula</a>, returning some of the material that formed the Sun to the interstellar medium.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<div class="references-small" style="-moz-column-width:60em; -webkit-column-width:60em; column-width:60em;margin-left: 1.5em;">
<ol type="a">
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Anone"><a href="#ref_Anone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Capitalization" title="Capitalization">Capitalization</a> of the name varies. The <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">IAU</a>, the authoritative body regarding astronomical nomenclature, specifies <a href="http://www.iau.org/public_press/themes/naming/" class="external text" rel="nofollow">capitalizing the names of all individual astronomical objects</a> (<b>Solar System</b>). However, the name is commonly rendered in lower case (<b>solar system</b>) - as, for example, in the <i><a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">Oxford English Dictionary</a></i>, <a href="http://www.m-w.com/dictionary/solar%20system" class="external text" rel="nofollow"><i>Merriam-Webster's 11th Collegiate Dictionary</i></a>, and <a href="http://www.britannica.com/eb/article-9110143" class="external text" rel="nofollow"><i>Encyclopædia Britannica</i></a>.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Bnone"><a href="#ref_Bnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> See <a href="/wiki/List_of_natural_satellites" title="List of natural satellites">List of natural satellites</a> for the full list of natural satellites of the eight planets and five dwarf planets.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Cnone"><a href="#ref_Cnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> The mass of the Solar System excluding the Sun, Jupiter and Saturn can be determined by adding together all the calculated masses for its largest objects and using rough calculations for the masses of the Oort cloud (estimated at roughly 3 Earth masses),<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup> the Kuiper belt (estimated at roughly 0.1 Earth mass)<sup id="cite_ref-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets_64-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Delsanti-Beyond_The_Planets-64"><span>[</span>65<span>]</span></a></sup> and the asteroid belt (estimated to be 0.0005 Earth mass)<sup id="cite_ref-Krasinsky2002_46-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Krasinsky2002-46"><span>[</span>47<span>]</span></a></sup> for a total, rounded upwards, of ~37 Earth masses, or 8.1 percent the mass in orbit around the Sun. With the combined masses of Uranus and Neptune (~31 Earth masses) subtracted, the remaining ~6 Earth masses of material comprise 1.3 percent of the total.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Dnone"><a href="#ref_Dnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> Astronomers measure distances within the Solar System in <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU). One AU equals the average distance between the centers of Earth and the Sun, or 149,598,000&#160;km. Pluto is about 38 AU from the Sun and Jupiter is about 5.2 AU from the Sun. One <a href="/wiki/Light-year" title="Light-year">light-year</a> is 63,240 AU.</li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Enone"><a href="#ref_Enone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> According to <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">current definitions</a>, objects in orbit around the Sun are classed dynamically and physically into three categories: <i>planets</i>, <i>dwarf planets</i> and <i>small Solar System bodies</i>. A <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planet</a> is any body in orbit around the Sun that has enough mass to form itself into a <a href="/wiki/Sphere" title="Sphere">spherical</a> shape and has <a href="/wiki/Cleared_the_neighbourhood" title="Cleared the neighbourhood" class="mw-redirect">cleared its immediate neighbourhood</a> of all smaller objects. By this definition, the Solar System has eight known planets: Mercury, Venus, Earth, Mars, Jupiter, Saturn, Uranus, and Neptune. Pluto does not fit this definition, as it has not cleared its orbit of surrounding Kuiper belt objects.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a> is a celestial body orbiting the Sun that is massive enough to be rounded by its own gravity but which has not cleared its neighbouring region of <a href="/wiki/Planetesimal" title="Planetesimal">planetesimals</a> and is not a satellite.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> By this definition, the Solar System has five known dwarf planets: Ceres, Pluto, Haumea, Makemake, and Eris.<sup id="cite_ref-name_70-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-name-70"><span>[</span>71<span>]</span></a></sup> Other objects may be classified in the future as dwarf planets, such as <a href="/wiki/90377_Sedna" title="90377 Sedna">Sedna</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> Dwarf planets that orbit in the trans-Neptunian region are called "<a href="/wiki/Plutoid" title="Plutoid">plutoids</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-IAU0804_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAU0804-116"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> The remainder of the objects in orbit around the Sun are <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">small Solar System bodies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FinalResolution_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FinalResolution-114"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li><span class="citation wikicite" id="endnote_Fnone"><a href="#ref_Fnone"><b><sup>^</sup></b></a></span> If ψ is the angle between the <a href="/wiki/Ecliptic_pole" title="Ecliptic pole">north pole of the ecliptic</a> and the north <a href="/wiki/Galactic_pole" title="Galactic pole" class="mw-redirect">galactic pole</a> then:
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd><span class="texhtml">cosψ = cos(β<sub><i>g</i></sub>)cos(β<sub><i>e</i></sub>)cos(α<sub><i>g</i></sub> − α<sub><i>e</i></sub>) + sin(β<sub><i>g</i></sub>)sin(β<sub><i>e</i></sub>)</span>,</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
where <span class="texhtml">β<sub><i>g</i></sub> =</span> 27° 07′ 42.01″ and <span class="texhtml">α<sub><i>g</i></sub> =</span> 12h 51m 26.282 are the declination and right ascension of the north galactic pole,<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> while <span class="texhtml">β<sub><i>e</i></sub> =</span> 66° 33′ 38.6″ and <span class="texhtml">α<sub><i>e</i></sub> =</span> 18h 0m 00 are those for the north pole of the ecliptic. (Both pairs of coordinates are for <a href="/wiki/J2000" title="J2000" class="mw-redirect">J2000</a> epoch.) The result of the calculatgion is 60.19°.</li>
</ol>
</div>
